From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Fri Jun  1 15:22:50 2007
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Fri, 01 Jun 2007 15:22:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Understanding DNS...
Message-ID: <1180736570.23935.34.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

I've been trying to study RFC 1035 and other relevant RFCs on
DNS because I'm busily writing a perl script to try and find
in my postfix mail log information about which SMTP clients
should be blacklisted.  I am only looking at 550 rejects where
the user is unknown in the relay recipient table right now.

I want to know things like, how do I understand discussions
of octets and practically determine from them how many 
characters a resolvable host name record must have?  

Why try to resolve a name that is malformed?  Net::DNS seems to 
be too slow for doing unnecessary DNS checks.

I want the following ideas I'm thinking I can use commented on:

* Resolvable fully qualified domain names are at least 1 domain and
  1 subdomain separated by one period.  I don't need to worry about
  other representations being used by legitimate smtp senders.

* I should force the same case when I compare one hostname to another.

* I can safely list (localhost, localhost.localdomain, unknown) as
  strings that should never be looked up via DNS.

* I can safely assume that the helo/ehlo and the RCPT from should
  be identical and consider an email more likely to be spam if they
  are not.

* No need to resolve the domains of sites I am hosting, but my partner
  is screwing up relaying mail which is targeted at mailbox names I 
  don't accept.

* I should blacklist the connect from IP address immediately of anyone
  who connects to my smtp relay trying to email a domain that I neither
  host nor provide backup service for.

* If you try to reach a mailbox name I have never hosted before that 
  doesn't exist 3 times, you are blacklisted.

* If you try to reach a mailbox name that I have phased out 6 times,
  you are blacklisted.

* If you try to use 2 different email names in a row that I don't serve,
  you are blacklisted.

The above ideas that I am curious about aside, another thing I want to
know about is how to set up a DNS blacklist.  Why not write zone files
directly and host them on my global DNS servers?  Is there a special
IP blacklist record in DNS?  The idea is that my partners can look
at my IP blacklist to determine who I will never accept mail from 
so that they don't try to relay email to me for spammers.

My basic concept is that there are rules I can check to figure out
when to blacklist people.  Graylisting isn't the best idea because
I seem to never know when a legit client won't retry and there's
the problem of not wanting to delay important email.

    Michael C. Robinson

From lnxknight at gmail.com  Sat Jun  2 05:55:46 2007
From: lnxknight at gmail.com (Matt McKenzie)
Date: Sat, 2 Jun 2007 05:55:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Fedora 7
In-Reply-To: <008801c7a4c2$b2d172b0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <008801c7a4c2$b2d172b0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <da2207740706020555s17737b47u21605d375eb8bab0@mail.gmail.com>

On 6/1/07, William A Morita <wamorita at hevanet.com> wrote:
>
> I recently downloaded Fedora 7 and burned it to DVD.
> I had also recently done some major updates on my FC6 system, but had not
> rebooted.
> When I rebooted my FC6 system, it would seem that the update had
> transformed
> my
> FC6 into a Fedora 7.
> No need for the DVD I just burned.
> With previous FC versions I never observed this type of upgrade
> phenomenon.
> I had always loaded the next version of FC into a different partition.


Unless something has been changed with the update structure unannounced, you
have to specifically and intentionally change the yum update sources in
order for a full version upgrade with yum.  Distro upgrades with yum are
possible but usually not recommended.  It doesn't always go as smoothly as
Debian and its children can do with "apt-get dist-upgrade".

If you just update normally it will not upgrade to a newer version of the
distro.  You can keep updating from the FC6 sources as long as they are
maintained.  This is the desirable behavior, you don't want your system to
automagically upgrade itself to a new version before you can test it, and
when you wake up in the morning things are borked.

Are you sure it didn't boot off the DVD and run live mode (like Knoppix)
when you rebooted?  FC7 now includes live mode so you can take it for a test
drive before installing, or use it for demos, etc.

I know there are live CDs distributed, but possibly the DVDs have live mode
as well.

I just downloaded the DVD but have not upgraded (or tried it live) yet, and
I also downloaded the live CD to try on other machines.

FWIW I run Fedora 6 AMD64 on my desktop right now, and Ubuntu Feisty on
several different laptops (PC and Mac), most all of them dual boot with
WinXP or Mac OS X.  I have been running Fedora since it first came out, and
before that was running RedHat since 6.0.  I have switched around with
various of the other distros on my other machines, but always kept RH or
Fedora on my main desktop.

-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

I just downloaded the DVD last night and as far as I can tell it does 
not have a "Live Mode". When it boots, it gives you a menu, including 
"Install", "Install Text Mode" and "Rescue CD", but I didn't see a live 
mode. Maybe I missed it. I'm currently installing it to a virtual 
machine under VMware Workstation 6.0.

Incidentally, one thing I miss in the installer for F7 (and CentOS 5 
too) is the ability to just load the whole enchilada with a single mouse 
click. You can customize the install and add stuff like you always 
could, but there's no "gimme it all I've got a huge hard drive" option 
any more.
>

Right now I'm on the hairy edge of switching to Fedora 7 for my main 
gateway (email/IRC/router/what I'm typing this on) box and my laptop, 
and staying with Gentoo on my high-performance dual-core big memory 
AMD64. It's mostly because Red Hat system administration is a marketable 
skill and Gentoo and Debian/Ubuntu system administration skills aren't, 
not because I think Fedora is "better" than Gentoo or Debian/Ubuntu.

On Sat, 02 Jun 2007 12:11:53 -0700
"M. Edward (Ed) Borasky" <znmeb at cesmail.net> dijo:

> > I know there are live CDs distributed, but possibly the DVDs have live 
> > mode as well.

> I just downloaded the DVD last night and as far as I can tell it does 
> not have a "Live Mode". When it boots, it gives you a menu, including 
> "Install", "Install Text Mode" and "Rescue CD", but I didn't see a live 
> mode. Maybe I missed it. I'm currently installing it to a virtual 
> machine under VMware Workstation 6.0.

The live mode is available only on the CD, not the DVD. However, the
live CD does present the option of loading the whole banana into RAM if
it senses you have enough RAM for it. As far as I know (and I just
tried out a lot of different distros on my new desktop), the Fedora 7
live CD is the only one with the ability to load the whole thing into
RAM. It's a nice touch, and one which I wish the other live CD distros
would copy.

I should also add that Fedora 7 is supposed to have an easy-to-use
facility for making your own custom live CDs. I never got far enough
into it to try it out, but it sounds like a cool feature.

There is a Live CD.  The Live CD can be used for doing installs by
copying the CD image to disk. It can't do installs.  The DVDs don't
have live mode.  There is a rescue CD for doing network installs.

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Sat Jun  2 21:45:43 2007

Coming from Red Hat 9, even the Red-Hat-like distros have evolved greatly in the
interim, but I think your Red Hat sysadmin skills will carry you further if you
stick with either Fedora 7 or CentOS 5. I've got Fedora 7 up and running and
it's much better in terms of completeness than some of the older Fedorae. It
has R, for example, available in the repository, a recent beta of the LyX
scientific word processor, and quite a bit more scientific software than the
older version s had.

But if you want stable, Etch or CentOS is the way to go. I've never used SuSE,
so I can't comment on it, but other folks have found it difficult to work with.

<target name="deploy" depends="init">
   <ftp server="halibut" binary="true" verbose="true"
      userid="ted" password="tedsSecretPassword" remotedir="${xferlocation}" >
      <fileset dir="${output}">
         <include name="**/*.zip"/>
      </fileset>
   </ftp>
</target>

From russell at personaltelco.net  Sun Jun  3 16:15:38 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 03 Jun 2007 16:15:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] SNMP problem
Message-ID: <86wsyk4okl.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


I am trying to solve a problem of sanely collecting some statistics
via SNMP.  I am using the NET-SNMP exec feature at the agent end, but
I want to collect multiple values that are tightly inter-related in
that they all get computed together.  If I have the agent exec script
return multiple values, they are returned as a string of multiple
values which I am pretty sure is going to confuse the hell out of
Cacti, the monitoring/graphing software I am using.

Here is the outlines of what I've done:

 a) I have a daemon on the monitored device periodically do a ping
    across a wireless link, and then if the ping succeeds, grab the
    rssi out of a /proc file and appends three values (time,
    ping latency, and rss) to a temporary file;

 b) set up an snmpd exec script that:

   i) moves the temporary file aside to avoid working on a changing
      file;

   ii) compute statistics from the moved file, min/max/ave latency,
       min/max/ave rssi and print them to stdout;

   iii) delete the moved file;

Again, this results in all the values appearing under one OID, which I
expect cacti will have difficulty with (maybe it won't?).  The problem
is, since step b(i) and b(iii) do somethings that should only be done
once per cycle, and since I don't know what order cacti is going to
grab OID's in, it isn't obvious how to have multiple exec scripts to
retrieve single values in a non-ugly way.

So, what is the right way to collect the multiple values?  I've
googled and haven't found any obvious solutions.  Clues most welcome.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

Sadly, this is the model Oracle seems to prefer.

Two ideas.  The first one is the better, simpler choice.  The second
one is more far-fetched.

You could use <scp/> along with authorized keys.  The administrator on
the remote host end would have control over who connects, since they
have the power to add and remove public keys.  No password would be
required.

Maybe mount the remote resource locally?  Perhaps you could change the
remote repository over from ftp to webdav, and then use davfs2/cadaver
to mount the resource to the local filesystem.  This would put the
security tokens in the hands of root.  Or, if the remote host is in
the local network, perhps nfs is good enough.

Carlos Konstanski


From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Sun Jun  3 16:38:11 2007
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Sun, 03 Jun 2007 16:38:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux Distro
In-Reply-To: <466345A9.2090206@frankhunt.com>
References: <4662DA3D.4010209@frankhunt.com>
	<20070603221141.GD31521@syrinxpc.com> <466345A9.2090206@frankhunt.com>
Message-ID: <1180913892.954.29.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

On Sun, 2007-06-03 at 18:50 -0400, Frank Hunt wrote:
> My info on Fedora is probably stale, but back in the FC2 & 3 days I seem 
> to recall that everything was not there.

Well, I don't think any Linux distro comes with Realplayer 
or webmail software.

The realplayer problem can be fixed I understand by adding 
helixplayer and mplayer plugins.  For webmail, I suggest 
the horde framework from http://www.horde.org and the imp 
package.

If you want a Redhatesqe system that is stable for a server, 
CentOS is a good choice.  If you don't need to run X, there 
is hardly any major differences between Redhateqse 
distributions, Debianesqe ones, and Slackware.

I want to see people get away from the idea that they need a
different distribution of Linux, because that fragments the 
Linux community a lot.  Anyone ever heard of PCLinux2007?
At one time there were evidently 300+ Linux distributions.
It wouldn't surprise me if there are twice that number now.

I fault the upgrade methods for all modern Linux distributions 
that are not offline repository friendly.  If Redhat 9 works 
and does everything you need it to, what on earth is wrong 
with patching whatever security holes it has that will be 
a problem for what you do with it and continuing to use it?

I would like to see yum redesigned so that it is easy to mirror 
repositories to a server on your local network and work from
that.  I am also concerned with so many updates for so many 
Linux distributions what these updates are for and whether or 
not they truly make things better.  There is something to be 
said for the upgrade a whole distribution at a time method.

The best way to avoid third party packages is to avoid owning
hardware that needs them.  It's a good idea to stay away from
64 bit hardware for a while.  I would like to see companies
release the specs to their advanced video cards and high end
USB/firewire hardware products under the GPL so that the OSS 
community can write high end OSS drivers.  When you need a 
proprietary driver on a Linux system, that is a major 
disadvantage.  These companies could collect donations via 
Paypal towards opening up their specs.  Name the price to 
open source your hardware, give the public a way to chip 
away at that, and see what happens.  Another option is to 
sell the traditional way with a certain portion of sales 
going towards open sourcing the device.  I think people will
pay a small premium if they know it's going towards making
their purchase more valuable in the long run.

    Michael C. Robinson



From blitters at gmail.com  Sun Jun  3 18:08:49 2007
From: blitters at gmail.com (Steve D...)
Date: Sun, 3 Jun 2007 18:08:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] SNMP problem
In-Reply-To: <86wsyk4okl.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86wsyk4okl.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <ff01ea180706031808k5abced92wf4dd1dd1a8cd183@mail.gmail.com>

On 03 Jun 2007 16:15:38 -0700, Russell Senior <russell at personaltelco.net> wrote:
>
> So, what is the right way to collect the multiple values?  I've
> googled and haven't found any obvious solutions.  Clues most welcome.

  I used to do something similar with a Cricket system I had set up.
I needed to collect the status data from a large number of
asynchronous SNMP TRAP messages.  The TRAP messages couldn't be made
to conform to RRDTool's rigid expectation of when and where data was
suppose to be located.  The trick was to separate out the data
creation and collection steps.

  I collected the TRAP data via an snmptrapd external script.  The
script would strip the OID data from the TRAP and appended it to a
flat file in the "cricket" user directory.  There was a separate flat
file for each device, or "target".  Each line in the file contained
multiple Data Definitions, or OID data.  The flat files would end up
being filled with multiple lines of status data from the routers.

  Cricket was configured to call an external program every (5 minute)
collection interval.  The external program would calculate the min,
max, average, and number of traps, from the data, and then erase the
file.  The min, max, average, and number of traps, would be returned
to RRDTool via std-out.

  The system worked out OK.  It wasn't correct from a mathematical
standpoint.  Graphing a average in RRDTool creates an average, of an
average, which is a sin.  Other than that, it was a pretty robust
collection system.

Steve D...

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Jun  3 22:23:49 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 3 Jun 2007 22:23:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux Distro
In-Reply-To: <20070603221141.GD31521@syrinxpc.com>
References: <4662DA3D.4010209@frankhunt.com>
	<20070603221141.GD31521@syrinxpc.com>

> On Sun, Jun 03, 2007 at 11:11:57AM -0400, Frank Hunt wrote:
> > I will be building a new server system soon and need a more current 
> > distro than the ancient RH9 on the current server.  I am looking for a 
> > pretty complete distribution with minimal assembly required (that 
> > eliminates the Fedoras and a few others).  

On Sun, Jun 03, 2007 at 03:11:41PM -0700, Aaron Burt wrote:
> I'd recommend Debian 4.0 or Centos 5.0, though any modern distro should
> support your hardware nicely and run the apps you want.

I must differ regards CentOS 5.0 ...

I am currently upgrading 7 machines (2 desktops, 2 servers, 2 laptops,
and a firewall) to an RHEL5 clone very similar to CentOS 5.0 .  I have
run into lots of small broken things.  Compared to a similar upgrade
on two machines to an RHEL 4.4 clone six months ago, it has been much
more painful and time consuming.  I imagine CentOS 5.1 will be a lot
prettier.  You might consider upgrading to CentOS 4.4, and upgrading
again at 5.1.  Another alternative to CentOS is Scientific Linux, 
which is a clone with extra scientific packages and some media stuff,
out of FermiLabs and CERN.

If I knew then what I know now, I probably would have moved everything
to 4.4.  But every bug I encounter I think is the last one.  Also, I
would have turned off SELINUX on everything inside the firewall.
SELINUX is nice in theory but breaks a lot of apps in actuality. 
Many are problems with recent versions of gnome, which are common
to many distros (Ubuntu edgy and feisty, for example). 

When I finish with my upgrades, I will post a list of problems 
encountered.  I am migrating one of the laptops from RH9, so there
may be some information we can trade.

Eventually, I think I will be happy with 5.0, but the journey has been
more painful than I planned on.

Keith

CentOS 5, and I haven't really taken the time to dig into it. There's

3. Fedora 7 looks really nice. I am struggling very hard with the 
temptation to join the Fedora bandwagon. But I have no intention of 
running anything but Gentoo on the new box, so I'm holding off.

> thoughts/ideas from the group.  I need to run the usual suspects

You may want to look at Ubuntu. I've been happy with the Ubuntu  
distro for the past 3 years. I previously used SUSE, but that was  
before they joined Novell, and that worked fine for me also. I think  
SUSE now costs money for their server edition, and I know that  
Ubuntu's sever edition is still free (i.e., no monetary cost).

From russell at personaltelco.net  Mon Jun  4 09:14:36 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 04 Jun 2007 09:14:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] SNMP problem
In-Reply-To: <ff01ea180706031808k5abced92wf4dd1dd1a8cd183@mail.gmail.com>
References: <86wsyk4okl.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<ff01ea180706031808k5abced92wf4dd1dd1a8cd183@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <86sl973deb.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Steve" == Steve D <blitters at gmail.com> writes:

Steve> On 03 Jun 2007 16:15:38 -0700, Russell Senior
Steve> <russell at personaltelco.net> wrote:
>>  So, what is the right way to collect the multiple values?  I've
>> googled and haven't found any obvious solutions.  Clues most
>> welcome.

Steve>   I used to do something similar with a Cricket system I had
Steve> set up.  [...]

Okay, I solved this.  I wrote a "get-value.sh" script that checks to
see if its value is fresh (less than 60 seconds old).  If not, it
calls a refreshing script, "calculate-stats.sh", which includes a
locking feature to prevent race conditions.  If a version is already
running, it waits until the lock goes away and then returns, otherwise
"calculate-stats.sh" computes all the values and stashes them in
separate files and returns.  The "get-values.sh" script then grabs its
value out of its file and prints that.  I can snmpget all 10 values in
about 2.5 seconds.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

On 04 Jun 2007 09:14:36 -0700, Russell Senior <russell at personaltelco.net> wrote:
> >>>>> "Steve" == Steve D <blitters at gmail.com> writes:
>
> Steve> On 03 Jun 2007 16:15:38 -0700, Russell Senior
> Steve> <russell at personaltelco.net> wrote:
> >>  So, what is the right way to collect the multiple values?  I've
> >> googled and haven't found any obvious solutions.  Clues most
> >> welcome.
>
> Steve>   I used to do something similar with a Cricket system I had
> Steve> set up.  [...]
>
> Okay, I solved this.  I wrote a "get-value.sh" script that checks to
> see if its value is fresh (less than 60 seconds old).  If not, it
> calls a refreshing script, "calculate-stats.sh", which includes a
> locking feature to prevent race conditions.  If a version is already
> running, it waits until the lock goes away and then returns, otherwise
> "calculate-stats.sh" computes all the values and stashes them in
> separate files and returns.  The "get-values.sh" script then grabs its
> value out of its file and prints that.  I can snmpget all 10 values in
> about 2.5 seconds.

  Be sure to test the failures.  The timeout values in NET-SNMP are
pretty long.  The time-outs with "ping" are even worse.  It's pretty
easy to code up a race condition if you have too many failures during
a collection interval.

  Ping also returns some pretty crazy text during different failure
conditions.  (try shutting down your ethernet port or munging your
route ;-) )  The "fping" application is a little more flexible that
the standard "ping" tool.  It's was also written (unlike ping) to be
called from a script.

Steve D...

>>>>> "Steve" == Steve D <blitters at gmail.com> writes:

Russell> checks to see if its value is fresh (less than 60 seconds
Russell> old).  If not, it calls a refreshing script,
Russell> "calculate-stats.sh", which includes a locking feature to

Steve>   Be sure to test the failures.  The timeout values in NET-SNMP
Steve> are pretty long.  The time-outs with "ping" are even worse.
Steve> It's pretty easy to code up a race condition if you have too
Steve> many failures during a collection interval.

Steve>   Ping also returns some pretty crazy text during different
Steve> failure conditions.  (try shutting down your ethernet port or
Steve> munging your route ;-) ) The "fping" application is a little
Steve> more flexible that the standard "ping" tool.  It's was also
Steve> written (unlike ping) to be called from a script.

Ah.  fping.  Didn't know about that.  Thanks!

The monitoring script runs on the device, like so:

   #!/bin/sh

   # grab information for link monitoring

   DESTIP=10.11.x.y
   DESTNAME=backhaul
   IFACE=ath2
   INTERVAL=500 # in 0.01 second units
   OUTDIR=/tmp/linkstats

   centiseconds () { 
   awk '{ printf("%ld\n", $1 * 100) }' /proc/uptime 
   }

   mkdir -p -m 777 ${OUTDIR}
   start=$(centiseconds)
   end=$start

   while true; do
       end=$(expr ${end} "+" ${INTERVAL})


       latency=$(ping -c 1 -i 5 -w 4 -q ${DESTIP} | sed -n -r -e 's|^rtt min/avg/max/mdev = ([0-9.]*)/.*|\1|p')

       if [ "${latency}" != "" ]; then
           rssi=$(awk 'NR == 1 { level = $1 } 
                       NR == 2 { noise = $1 } 
                       END { print level - noise }' /sys/class/net/${IFACE}/wireless/level /sys/class/net/${IFACE}/wireless/noise)
       else
           latency=""
           rssi=0
       fi

       now=$(centiseconds)
       echo $now $latency $rssi >> ${OUTDIR}/${DESTNAME}

       sleep $(expr $(expr ${end} "-" ${now}) "/" 100)
   done

Looks like the latency assignment could be shortened to:

  latency=$(fping -A -C 1 -t 4000 -q ${DESTIP} | cut -d' ' -f3)

The clock stuff is a hack job, that could be made nicer.  During the
computation of statistics, the file that is accumulating here is mv'd
to a "work area" so as not to interfere with new incoming values.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net


From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Mon Jun  4 13:10:51 2007
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Mon, 04 Jun 2007 20:10:51 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] How many open source OS projects exist?
Message-ID: <1180987851.7696.63.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

I found out there is an AROS project which supposedly grew
out of the AOS project.  It is an attempt to produce the
equivalent of Wine for AmigaOS.

I found out about AROS trying to google on the question 
of whether or not there are alternatives to ReactOS.  
ReactOS appears to be stuck in, 
"becoming compatible with the threading capabilities 
of Windows 2000/XP hell."

Maybe ReactOS has gone backwards in the 0.3.x releases so
far due to the audit, because of attempts to rewrite kernel 
code that is hard to account for.  I've read that there is
difficulty created by people who contribute code to the 
ReactOS project only to leave without providing any means
to contact them in the future.

The two month release time line after ReactOS 0.3.1
is looking more like a 4 month long one.  I wouldn't
want to be responsible for ReactOS's web site because 
developers got angry when it didn't represent the 
development time line accurately and people asked 
about the discrepancy on the discussion boards.  I 
asked on the NEWS board what is going on 
( I didn't know any better at the time. ).

AROS doesn't do memory protection and it's web site says
that it's impossible to do memory protection for it.  The
FAQ also says that arguing about adding certain modern
features to AROS that AmigaOS doesn't have is pointless
until something stable is produced.  This at least
suggests that AROS is easier to stabilize than ReactOS
in the short run.  But is AROS worth anything?  There
aren't any modern Amiga's being made to my knowledge
even if the Amiga's idea of plug and play really does
work.

For ReactOS, I wish the developers could find a way to 
keep it from crashing if a feature isn't implemented yet.
I suppose part of the problem is having to implement the
GUI in ring 0 of the Intel processor spec because 
Microsoft does this with Windows.  An OS that is very
protective when an unfamiliar binary wants to execute
that can back off when it knows the binary works could
have a major advantage over the current OS choices.  
Unfortunately, a kernel with this capability would be 
simply huge I imagine.

Why is the idea of creating a minimal GUI environment 
to support Firefox on top of Freedos controversial?
If I want to run Firefox in a virtual environment
as a means to protect my host system, do I really
need the average Linux or Windows environment for 
that?  Freedos has the ability to run on old 
equipment stably than even Linux can't run on.  
In theory, a lite Firefox gui for Freedos can 
bring the hardware requirements to run Firefox 
down.

I suppose 800+ Mhz systems that can run Firefox smoothly
are so common that noone cares about running it on lower
spec machines.

Are there any groups trying to build an OS and encourage
production of hardware for it that is the best of every
operating system that came before it?  What would the
best of: Linux, Windows, BSD, MacOS-X, and AmigaOS look
like?

   --  Michael C. Robinson

in the short run.  But is AROS worth anything?  There
aren't any modern Amiga's being made to my knowledge
even if the Amiga's idea of plug and play really does
work.

For ReactOS, I wish the developers could find a way to 
keep it from crashing if a feature isn't implemented yet.
I suppose part of the problem is having to implement the
GUI in ring 0 of the Intel processor spec because 
Microsoft does this with Windows.  An OS that is very
protective when an unfamiliar binary wants to execute
that can back off when it knows the binary works could
have a major advantage over the current OS choices.  
Unfortunately, a kernel with this capability would be 
simply huge I imagine.

Why is the idea of creating a minimal GUI environment 
to support Firefox on top of Freedos controversial?
If I want to run Firefox in a virtual environment
as a means to protect my host system, do I really
need the average Linux or Windows environment for 
that?  Freedos has the ability to run on old 
equipment stably than even Linux can't run on.  
In theory, a lite Firefox gui for Freedos can 
bring the hardware requirements to run Firefox 
down.

I suppose 800+ Mhz systems that can run Firefox smoothly
are so common that noone cares about running it on lower
spec machines.

Are there any groups trying to build an OS and encourage
production of hardware for it that is the best of every
operating system that came before it?  What would the
best of: Linux, Windows, BSD, MacOS-X, and AmigaOS look
like?

   --  Michael C. Robinson

Subject: [PLUG] Linux Distro

	<174E313F-5C34-42F8-B979-616BACC85660@scottshill.com>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0706042101qf9c7a08gb9b56c4598b8f015@mail.gmail.com>

On 6/4/07, Kenneth B. Hill <ken at scottshill.com> wrote:
>
> On Jun 3, 2007, at 8:11 AM, Frank Hunt wrote:
>
> > I will be building a new server system soon and need a more current
> > distro than the ancient RH9 on the current server.  I am looking
> > for a pretty complete distribution with minimal assembly required
> > (that eliminates the Fedoras and a few others).  I also am on a
> > very limited budget (that means I need FREE or almost free).  Right
> > now I am leaning toward either Debian or SuSE.  Wanted to get some
> > thoughts/ideas from the group.  I need to run the usual suspects
> > (Apache, postfix, spamassassin, squirrelmail).  Must support SATA
> > as a secondary drive. Hardware is a Dell OptiPlex GX270 with
> > integrated Intel 865G video.


I run a number of servers on Ubuntu Dapper and find it to be very nice.
Canonical is committed to supporting Dapper on servers until 2012, and it is
new enough (released in June of 2006) that the software hasn't gotten stale.
If you need newer packages than are available on Dapper, you can use Feisty
(7.04) but it will only be supported for 18 months, IIRC. I agree with
another poster that once you get used to apt-get (or aptitude, in my
particular case) everything else out there (except maybe foresight's
Conary...) seems archaic in comparison. Regardless of whether you use Debian
or Ubuntu, I'd strongly recommend either, based mostly on the fact that APT
is so nice to use.

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Mon Jun  4 23:57:01 2007
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Mon, 4 Jun 2007 23:57:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RHEL 5
In-Reply-To: <4664FDEC.2070806@gmail.com>
References: <4664FDEC.2070806@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0706042357m30244cak2b50bd906e50ca3@mail.gmail.com>

Both Scientific Linux 5 and CentOS 5 are analogs (minus encumbered
images) of RHEL5, recompiled from source.  CentOS seems to follow a
little more quickly, while STL has (or allows in some national lab
derivatives) some additional features/packages.

The closest Fedora would be 5, with some similarities to 6... or
vise-versa... not "directly" related at all, details on fedora's site,
from Redhat, etc, and they vary with time ;)

/eli

I.E.
rpm -ivh --aid foo.rpm

From griffint at pobox.com  Tue Jun  5 12:01:45 2007
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Tue, 5 Jun 2007 12:01:45 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] RPM commands
In-Reply-To: <727fc2ee0706051122o75f8ab39r979bc7f57b8a4ac4@mail.gmail.com>
References: <727fc2ee0706051122o75f8ab39r979bc7f57b8a4ac4@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <58635.10.8.0.2.1181070105.squirrel@chinook>

On Tue, June 5, 2007 11:22 am, User Iam wrote:
> HI
>
>
> Doing some RH5 stuff..
>
>
> Need to install some rpms
>
>
> So I do rpm -i <package name>
>
>
> and get about 6 dependencies..
>
> I was thinking that there was a command that would install the deps....
> automatically??
>
> I can't seem to find it on the man page..
>
>
> And I am not on the net.. Installing from an NFS share..
>
>

Here's one way:

1. Install the createrepo package (on the client side or the NFS server).

2. Run createrepo on your NFS directory containing the RPMs.

3. On the client edit the files in directory /etc/yum.repos.d.
- Add "enabled=0" line for the repositories you don't want or have.
- For those you want and have:
   - Add "enabled=1" line (or just remove the line).
   - Comment-out any existing mirrorlist or baseurl lines.
   - Add your own baseurl line, something like:
      baseurl=file:///nfs-mount/path/to/rpm/repository

4. Run "yum install packagename"

Terry

> > Need to install some rpms
> >
> >
> > So I do rpm -i <package name>
> >
> >
> > and get about 6 dependencies..
> >
> > I was thinking that there was a command that would install the deps....
> > automatically??
> >
> > I can't seem to find it on the man page..
> >
> >
> > And I am not on the net.. Installing from an NFS share..
> >
> >
>
> Here's one way:
>
> 1. Install the createrepo package (on the client side or the NFS server).
>
> 2. Run createrepo on your NFS directory containing the RPMs.
>
> 3. On the client edit the files in directory /etc/yum.repos.d.
> - Add "enabled=0" line for the repositories you don't want or have.
> - For those you want and have:
>    - Add "enabled=1" line (or just remove the line).
>    - Comment-out any existing mirrorlist or baseurl lines.
>    - Add your own baseurl line, something like:
>       baseurl=file:///nfs-mount/path/to/rpm/repository
>
> 4. Run "yum install packagename"
>
> Terry



Thanks

This worked great..

User Iam

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Jun  5 21:42:31 2007
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 5 Jun 2007 22:42:31 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] java accounting math
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706052153050.24526@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I am starting to code the math portion of a java accounting app at Ye
Olde Day Job.  It is a refactor of a badly designed death-jsp that
some contractor wrote a few years back.  The important things to know
about this app are that it's java, it has to do accounting math
accurately, and I have the freedom to put forth extra effort to make
it nicely written, maintainable and mathematically accurate, since
everyone's sick of dealing with the contractor-code.

I'm fishing for best practices in the area of programming accounting
apps in java.  I have been able to find very little online.  All I
found so far was this bug report, which says it's better to use new
BigDecimal(String) than new BigDecimal(double):

http://bugs.sun.com/bugdatabase/view_bug.do?bug_id=4863883

Should BigDecimal be used for java penny-crunching, or is Double
sufficient?  Doing math with BigDecimal sure is a pain, and it looks
nothing like math.  Maintainability of the source code is a big
priority.  Easy-to-read formulae are a bonus.

In this vein, I am looking at jatha.  Has anyone used it?  Expressing
the math in lisp would be the pinnacle of elegance.  The big question
is the data type returned by math functions.
org.jatha.dynatype.StandardLispReal has a toJava() method, which
"Returns a Java Double, Float or Integer object, depending on the
typeHint."

The BigDecimal versus Double question is the one I have to deal with
first, since everything depends on it.  So if I could ask only one
question per post, this would be it.

Carlos Konstanski


From russell at personaltelco.net  Tue Jun  5 21:45:49 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 05 Jun 2007 21:45:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] how to force a reboot with no working rootfs?
Message-ID: <86bqftlmgy.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


I know you are all waiting anxiously for the answer to this question I
brought up over a year ago, so I won't keep you in suspense any
longer.

Around May 3, last year, I asked a question about how to force a
reboot when you had just stomped all over the read-only filesystem you
had just moments before been running off of.  I was investigating ways
to remotely flash an embedded wireless router (Soekris 4826 boards)
from a running system.  Remotely flashing was desirable, because they
were strapped atop scary roofs.  Remotely rebooting was desirable
because I don't always have access to the power supply and because I
am too lazy to leave the house.

Basically, I ssh into the box, pipeline a file from over the network
(with ssh or wget) and /bin/dd it directly onto a 64meg soldered-on
flash device with an ide interface, /dev/hda.  Something like:

  ssh <user>@<host> 'dd if=new-image.img' | dd of=/dev/hda bs=64k

Since /dev/hda1 is what the system is running off of (read-only),
the system gets severely confused after the dd has completed.  If the
new image shares enough structure with the old image, it may still be
possible that you can type "shutdown -r now" and it'll work.  I was
encountering a situation where it didn't, and I was trying to figure a
way to do the remote reboot, as the reboot solves all.

I got a few suggestions, which either were not supported on the
running system, or which actually just didn't work, or which I was
just too stupid to notice and follow up on.  Last year, I gave up and
waited to get physical access to the power cord.

Flash forward to the present.  I am working on some fresh firmware to
solve various accumulated problems with the old firmware and I find
myself, again, too lazy to leave the house.  I think to myself *THERE
MUST BE A WAY*.

I was thinking about some kind of pivot_root scheme where I'd set up
enough of a system in a ramdisk that I could still run a few commands
after the flash was turned to mush under my feet.  Turns out, that
doesn't work.  It needs /dev/initctl (a fifo) to talk to init to do
the shutdown, which after you chroot isn't available any more.  I
tried a few other things, before staggering back to the obvious.  How
does init reboot?  Turns out there is a binary /bin/halt (and a
symlink to it called /bin/reboot) that takes a -f force option.  I
download some source and read.  Oh.  There is a system call, reboot,
with a magic number.  I write a very short program, that I call
poison.c:

  #include <sys/reboot.h>
  int main () { reboot(RB_AUTOBOOT); }

and link it statically (gcc -o poison -static poison.c) so that I
don't have to worry about the libraries that no longer exist after the
flash.  The binary is about 400k, btw, even stripped, but no matter,
it is well worth every lovely 100 kilobytes.  So, now I ssh in, copy
in the poison binary to a ramdisk (there will be no copying or much of
anything else after the flash), do my ssh pipeline to dd, and as soon
as it's complete, I bite down on the capsule (run ./poison) and wait
for reincarnation (get the remote reboot I have been long longing
for).

Ahhh.

Now, I *am* seeing some corruption in the reflash that I hadn't
noticed before.  After the reboot, I md5sum /dev/hda and get the wrong
number, but that's another story for another day.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

> plain cents, do your math, and convert the result back to dollars and cents

This app is not just pennies, actually.  I forgot to mention that
there is also percentages.  The ratio of budgeted labor hours
vs. actual labor hours is one example.  These invariably involve
dividing two large numbers (large in terms of dollars, anyway).
However, if I am careful, I can avoid doing calculations with these
percentages.  They should always be the output of a calculation, never
the input into one.

I'm not too fond of representing a typed value with a different data
type, with lots of conversion back and forth.  However, there is
something to be said for it if I gain accuracy, and the conversions
can be abstracted away.  Binary Coded Decimal is a prime example.  I
doubt it's best practice to implement BCD in most java accounting
programs.  Is it more common to see Integer representations?

Carlos Konstanski


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Jun  6 06:27:23 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 6 Jun 2007 06:27:23 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] java accounting math
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706052153050.24526@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706052153050.24526@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706060620530.13506@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tue, 5 Jun 2007, Carlos Konstanski wrote:

> I'm fishing for best practices in the area of programming accounting apps
> in java.  I have been able to find very little online. All I found so far
> was this bug report, which says it's better to use new BigDecimal(String)
> than new BigDecimal(double):

Carlos,

   I don't know java, but I've dealt with this issue before in C and Python.
In general, using a string or long integer is preferable to using
floats/reals.

   The problem with the latter is the way they are represented internally as
binary digits. To illustrate, try this in your python shell:

>>> 125.49 - 53.16
72.329999999999998

and imagine how this very small lack of accuracy will cascade through a
series of transactions.

   Many languages have libraries to handle high-precision monetary (and
similar) arithmetic using integers or strings. It appears that the
BigDecimal(String) library is what java offers. While it may be more effort
to write, the results are certainly worth it.

HTH,

Rich

1. There is a project called Tunes that is attempting to "redefine 
computing". Their web site, surprisingly, is http://tunes.org.

2. If you're going to look at historical operating systems, you might 
want to include some mainframe and superminicomputer operating systems, 
such as VAX/VMS, Xerox CP-V/Honeywell CP-6 and the venerable IBM 
juggernauts like MVS and VM.

3. This question probably belongs on Plug-Talk rather than here, but 
I'll ask it here anyway. What is this fascination people have with being 
able to run on older machines? If you look at the MIPS per watt or MIPS 
per cubic foot of, say, an AMD Athlon64 X2 4200+, compared with two 1 
GHz systems, four 500 MHz systems, etc., you really have to wonder why 
people hang on to their old boxes.

I gave away my 933 MHz Pentium III to free up a slot on my 2-port KVM 
switch for the X2. I am now thinking I'll give away the other box on the 
KVM switch, a 1.3 GHz Athlon TBird.

I haven't looked at ant in a while, but I have a question and a guess.

- Why not use a <zip> task instead of an exec task with zip? Then you
can define a fileset or zipfileset the way you expect

- Guess: When you use exec, ant is forking the executable directly, so
there's no shell executed to interpret the * as a fileglob. Instead,
it's being passed as a literal splat character.

Good luck!

-Alex

From tkubaska at charter.net  Wed Jun  6 10:02:16 2007
From: tkubaska at charter.net (Ted Kubaska)
Date: Wed, 06 Jun 2007 10:02:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] zip inside an antfile ... strange
In-Reply-To: <1d065ed40706060846t2b03bc19l969b291f3129cd09@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1783877232.1181102119214.JavaMail.root@fepweb11>
	<1d065ed40706060846t2b03bc19l969b291f3129cd09@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1181149336.5506.29.camel@halibut>

Thanks. Why the exec? We never get to write anything from scratch these
days .. this code snippet is from a build environment that contains
hundreds of xml lines and dozens of config files (it actually runs under
CruiseControl and uses Eclipse's antRunner). Using exec has something to
do with retaining file permissions in the zip (maybe). I did think of
using the zip task ... but I wanted to change as little as possible
(also took out the -q to put more info into our log file).

It is working now. I changed some lines in one of the xml files to be 

<exec executable="zip" dir="${assemblyTempDir}/">
  <arg line="-r ${zipargs} '${archiveFullPath}'"/>
  <arg line="${pluginArchivePrefix} -xi
${pluginArchivePrefix}/**/*.jar"/>
</exec>

instead of 

<exec executable="zip" dir="${assemblyTempDir}">
  <arg line="-r -q ${zipargs} '${archiveFullPath}'"/>
  <arg line="${pluginArchivePrefix}/*.jar"/>
</exec>

This was a "seat of the pants" change. I don't know why it works. It's
not how I interpret the docs. I'd like eventually to understand what's
happening, and it's something I'll monitor. But at least the builds are
working now.

 -ted

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Wed Jun  6 10:32:32 2007
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Wed, 6 Jun 2007 11:32:32 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] java accounting math
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706060620530.13506@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706052153050.24526@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0706060620530.13506@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <50376.127.0.0.1.1181151152.squirrel@webmail.pippiandcarlos.com>

> On Tue, 5 Jun 2007, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
>
>> I'm fishing for best practices in the area of programming accounting
>> apps
>> in java.  I have been able to find very little online. All I found so
>> far
>> was this bug report, which says it's better to use new
>> BigDecimal(String)
>> than new BigDecimal(double):
>
> Carlos,
>
>    I don't know java, but I've dealt with this issue before in C and
> Python.
> In general, using a string or long integer is preferable to using
> floats/reals.
>
>    The problem with the latter is the way they are represented internally
> as
> binary digits. To illustrate, try this in your python shell:
>
>>>> 125.49 - 53.16
> 72.329999999999998
>
> and imagine how this very small lack of accuracy will cascade through a
> series of transactions.
>
>    Many languages have libraries to handle high-precision monetary (and
> similar) arithmetic using integers or strings. It appears that the
> BigDecimal(String) library is what java offers. While it may be more
> effort
> to write, the results are certainly worth it.
>
> HTH,
>
> Rich

It appears that you are correct in presuming that BigDecimal is java's way
of handling accounting arithmetic.  I found a Sun java API for smart card
programming, which contains a class called BigNumber, which encapsulates
BCD representation.  But it looks far too chunky to use in an accounting
app, while BigDecimal seems adequate for the task.

In a world where languages exist that can easily handle the math (fortran,
lisp, caml), why do we use java for accounting anyway?  What a marketing
triumph.  Maybe we should rename lisp after an addictive beverage, and
then everyone will want it.  I propose "Urquell".

Carlos Konstanski



From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Jun  6 11:02:34 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 6 Jun 2007 11:02:34 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] java accounting math
In-Reply-To: <50376.127.0.0.1.1181151152.squirrel@webmail.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706052153050.24526@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0706060620530.13506@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<50376.127.0.0.1.1181151152.squirrel@webmail.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0706061052500.13506@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 6 Jun 2007, Carlos Konstanski wrote:

> It appears that you are correct in presuming that BigDecimal is java's way
> of handling accounting arithmetic.  I found a Sun java API for smart card
> programming, which contains a class called BigNumber, which encapsulates
> BCD representation.  But it looks far too chunky to use in an accounting
> app, while BigDecimal seems adequate for the task.

Carlos,

   I remember struggling to learn BCD for use in FORTRAN IV with the WATFOR
compiler on main frames. Those IBM manuals were terrible!

> In a world where languages exist that can easily handle the math (fortran,
> lisp, caml), why do we use java for accounting anyway?  What a marketing
> triumph.  Maybe we should rename lisp after an addictive beverage, and
> then everyone will want it.  I propose "Urquell".

   Well, here's something to think about: use java for the middleware and
front end (with SWING), but use PostgreSQL (or SQLite3) for the back end.
They have data types (NUMERIC and DECIMAL, with postgres; INTEGER in sqlite)
with sufficient precision, but you need ancillary functions to make them
work like the deprecated MONEY type in postgres. But, storing the accounting
data in tables makes present and future maintenance so much easier.

   If you want to look at a perl example for ideas, look at SQL-Ledger
<http://www.sql-ledger.org/>. You might be able to port it from perl to java
to meet your company's needs.

Rich

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Wed Jun  6 13:39:16 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Wed, 6 Jun 2007 13:39:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] striping in LVM or md?
Message-ID: <200706061339.17251.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

Hi all,

I've got four SATA disks and want to setup a raid 10 with md.

I'll be using md to create two mirror pairs.

I'm trying to decide whether I'm better off using lvm or md for the 
striping.  From my reading, lvm will parallelize writes across pv's, 
but I can't find any sort of definitive answer as to whether md is 
significantly faster at striping than lvm.  If the answer is yes, then 
I guess I build the whole thing with md.

But if there is little or no performance difference between md and lvm 
striping, then I have to wonder whether lvm should be involved.

It looks like managing a collection of disks as one logical volume is 
just as manageable with md as with lvm.  Where lvm comes into play is 
if I decide to allocate multiple LV's on a single PV.  That is, it 
seems that striping would be faster as pure-lvm if I am already using 
lvm to allocate multiple LV's (because it eliminates one layer of md), 
but assuming no logical volume subdivisions, I would guess that a 
purely md setup would prevail.

Summarizing, I know I'm going to create these mirrors with md:

  A:= md raid1 of disks sda and sdb
  B:= md raid1 of disks sdc and sdd

Then I want to stripe them together as either:

  V:= md raid0 of A+B
or
  V:= lvm VG of A+B

And I'm pondering possibly

  V1:= lvm-defined LV
  V2:= lvm-defined LV
  (etc.)

But if I do make LV's, then the md raid0 is an extra layer:

  A:= md raid1 sda+sdb
  B:= md raid1 sdc+sdd
  V:= md raid0 A+B
  V':= lvm VG of V
  V1:= lvm LV of V'
  V2:= lvm LV of V'

vs using md only for the mirroring:

  A:= md raid1 sda+sdb
  B:= md raid1 sdc+sdd
  V:= lvm VG of V
  V1:= lvm LV of V
  V2:= lvm LV of V

vs the "maybe I only need one mount point anyway" md-only answer:

  A:= md raid1 sda+sdb
  B:= md raid1 sdc+sdd
  V:= md raid0 A+B
  mount V

(though this stripe-to-single-lv could also be done in lvm)

So, what's the best performance?  How much flexibility/functionality do 
I lose to do without lvm?  I've been using lvm for a while (strictly in 
"bag of disks with multiple lv's" mode), but this is my first forray 
into md and I've decided that it's probably time for some redundancy.

Because I have data on these disks and need to keep the server going, I 
can't exactly take an afternoon and benchmark it each way without 
spending all of the time moving data around and probably dropping 
something (I have backups, but some of this is just big deb/cpan 
mirrors and other various stuff that I would rather not juggle/re-pull 
from the net today.)  Thus, I'm seeking some best-guess 
recommendations.

Thanks,
Eric
-- 
Atavism  n:  The recurrence of any peculiarity or disease of an ancestor
in a subsequent generation, usually due to genetic recombination.
---------------------------------------------------
    http://scratchcomputing.com
---------------------------------------------------


From eli.stair at gmail.com  Wed Jun  6 14:00:59 2007
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Wed, 6 Jun 2007 14:00:59 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] striping in LVM or md?
In-Reply-To: <200706061339.17251.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200706061339.17251.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0706061400n2a483385we2ec5444b170b0fb@mail.gmail.com>

In short, I'd suggest using the md-raid10 module.  It handles in one
module/management point the striping/mirroring.  I've had exceptional
performance using this with 14-drive FC arrays.  Feel free to layer
LVM on top.  As for using LVM methods for striping, it definitely has
its place (I use LVM striping to aggregate 28 RAID5 luns into a single
GFS filesystem...), but in a single installation with four disks, IMO
it's not appropriate when it can be handled more simply/cleanly in the
underlying layer that you're creating the array at.

You can of course implement things several ways, as you've noted... If
you don't have time to bench/test/validate everything, I'd suggest
going with the simplest-to-deploy method, that results in being
simplest to debug as well ;)


/eli

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Wed Jun  6 20:30:14 2007
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Wed, 6 Jun 2007 20:30:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] striping in LVM or md?
In-Reply-To: <200706062001.51270.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200706061339.17251.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<f4468cae0706061400n2a483385we2ec5444b170b0fb@mail.gmail.com>
	<200706062001.51270.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0706062030p6b4aeedfs6027c4df9f0c1951@mail.gmail.com>

On 6/6/07, Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com> wrote:
> # from Eli Stair
> # on Wednesday 06 June 2007 02:00 pm:
>
> >On 6/6/07, Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com> wrote:
> >> I've got four SATA disks and want to setup a raid 10 with md.
> >>
> >> I'll be using md to create two mirror pairs.
>
> >In short, I'd suggest using the md-raid10 module.  It handles in one
> >module/management point the striping/mirroring.
>
> I forgot to mention that I have a pair of 120GB disks and one pair of
> 160GB disks.  Does that change the answer.
>
> On further reading, it sounds like md wants similar-sized devices for
> raid0, though I guess I could do "linear" on top of raid1?

Any method other than JBOD will lose space in excess of the
lower-common-denominator disk, so you'll end up with a raid of 4x120G
(240G partitioned).  JBOD will give you a linear increase in volume
size based on #*size of disks, with a corresponding increase in
likelihood of full-filesystem-failure due to any one disk error
trashing the whole filesystem/volume.

> >Feel free to layer LVM on top.
>
> Wouldn't that cost me just as much (or more) performance than doing the
> striping in LVM?  That's mainly what I was wondering with the original
> question.  i.e. if I'm doing LVM anyway, it couldn't possibly be
> without cpu cost, so shouldn't I just mirror in md and then make a VG
> out of md0 and md1?

Assuming you use md-raid10, you wouldn't use any striping/allocation
features of LVM, just saying you could still use it for the ability to
resize later (if you add disks/resize the array underneath).  That
type of usage adds no processing overhead for any operations.

>
> Anybody got any references to benchmarks on that?  All google gives me
> is questions.
>
> Thanks,
> Eric
> --
> "You can't win. You can't break even. You can't quit."
> --Ginsberg's Restatement of the Three Laws of Thermodynamics

From danielmyoung at gmail.com  Wed Jun  6 21:13:36 2007

	<f4468cae0706061400n2a483385we2ec5444b170b0fb@mail.gmail.com>

Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0706062113m5fc5f372ta2f4297740fca177@mail.gmail.com>

On 6/6/07, Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com> wrote:
> Wouldn't that cost me just as much (or more) performance than doing the
> striping in LVM?  That's mainly what I was wondering with the original
> question.  i.e. if I'm doing LVM anyway, it couldn't possibly be
> without cpu cost, so shouldn't I just mirror in md and then make a VG
> out of md0 and md1?
>
> Anybody got any references to benchmarks on that?  All google gives me
> is questions.

At work we've got a big LVM VG of md RAID1 pairs. The performance is
effectively the same as just a single RAID1 array, except where I/O
happens to different PVs at the same time, in which case, it's, um,
faster...

Anecdotally, it eats the RAID5 array it replaced for lunch. Also has
the advantage of being easy to add space; just add a new RAID1 pair
and vgextend, etc.

Is your I/O load expected to be read-heavy?

-- 
Dan

No, otherwise how would you bring it back up again.  To prevent
routing from getting confused on the downed interface, though, you can
do an "ifconfig eth0 0.0.0.0".

User> #2... How do I reset the count on TX and RX buffers?

User> missed it...  <this happens a lot ... don't know what I am
User> looking for..>

Figure out which module it is and rmmod and modprobe it.  That's one
way, anyway.

From dwight at dwightandamy.com  Thu Jun  7 11:02:16 2007

Subject: [PLUG] Socket question
In-Reply-To: <46680EEA.5050100@acm.org>
References: <46673EEA.1040005@acm.org>
	<321871a60706061720h1bc700b8la01cfa89b32f8d35@mail.gmail.com>
	<46680EEA.5050100@acm.org>
Message-ID: <321871a60706071102n3b1f97e7n1cd048f869166dfb@mail.gmail.com>

If you sshed into the remote system from say 192.168.0.1 and you are doing a
test connection from another host you can use the not construct for tcpdump
to remove all the traffic from the host your are connecting from.  I.E.:
tcpdump not host 192.168.0.1

Or if you are connected via ssh, you can filter out your ssh (port 22)
traffic fairly easily:
tcpdump not port 22

You could also specify the port you are trying to connect to but you would
On 6/7/07, Roderick A. Anderson <raanders at acm.org> wrote:
>

>> Thanks, that's basically what it looks like I'm going to do. ?I was

>
>I'd guess mostly linear if you're starting from empty PVs, except

The LVM manpage implies that my choices for allocation policy are 
"normal" or "one of the unimplemented types".  :-D

On further experimentation, I see that I can lvcreate with -i 2 for 
striping *up to* the least common size, but lvextend will allow "-i 1" 
to add the rest of the available space without striping.  So, there's 
the "mostly striped" functionality that I was wanting.  I'll guess that 
it will fill the striped areas first.

According to the docs, this mixed striping is only supported in LVM2.

Quick update and a thank you to all that offered suggestions.

It was just where I thought the problem was.  The remote software. 
Turns out we weren't the first to have the problem.  Another UNIX 
installation had the same problem.

We as in, the software/service vendor and where I work, have come up 
with a work around that doesn't involve the socket based connection API.

From tkubaska at charter.net  Fri Jun  8 10:30:47 2007
From: tkubaska at charter.net (tkubaska at charter.net)
Date: Fri, 8 Jun 2007 10:30:47 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] headless builds and automatically generated files
Message-ID: <407983231.1181323847418.JavaMail.root@fepweb14>

On a more general level, here is what I'm struggling with ... wondering if other people have the same issues. I mean if you have the same problem, do you approach it in the same way (it seems to me there's got to be a better way).

I'm doing these Ecipse headless builds. Headless in this context means using Eclipse's PDE builder but not using it through the IDE.  This part is standard in the Ecipse world. People do headless builds (sometimes on the command line as an operator, other times through scripts), and end up with a bunch of jar files that they then deploy. If you're building a lot, you don't want to spend your time clicking through windows; you want to run a script.

Eclipse (the program) provides a lot of support to do this. Bascially what you do is set up a bunch of parameters and then push the button. Most of the files (a lot of them are ant files) that get run are automatically generated.

It turns out that you give Eclipse a parameter to tell it want files to get from its CVS repository. This can be the name of a tag or just HEAD. All the code to check out the files is automatically generated (and you can't edit it, because the next time you run, these automatically generated files are deleted and made again). 

Now the code that makes the jar files is also automatically generated. Each Eclipse plugin gets its own generated build.xml. Inside that build.xml is a <jar/> task that makes the jar files. What I'm generating has dozens of generated build.xml files and hence dozens of jar files.

The automatic system uses the same parameter to construct the name of the jar file it makes that it does when it checks out the files. If you set this parameter to what you want the jar files to be named, the CVS checkout fails because there are no files in CVS tagged with that name.

What I want to do (I think I want to do this, but if it's too strange then I want to do what other people do) is check out the HEAD (because the HEAD should always be buildable and if it isn't I want to know), and build the HEAD. If the HEAD builds I want to zip up the resulting jar files and stick them on a site for download by our users. I don't want these jar files with HEAD in their names. Rather I want their names to have a unique name (like a timestamp, preferable the timestamp when I started the build, which mostly takes under 10 minutes). 

For a while I thought I wanted to get the latest tagged version, but I don't think so anymore. The tagged versions are our releases and interim realeases, not our daily builds. So they always build (I discreetly coughed here). I don't think I want to save and tag our daily builds even when they work. 

Here is a question ... do people save and tag daily builds that are just daily builds? You'd end up with a lot of tagged versions in CVS. A build only occurs if the CVS changes, but in this environment that is just about every day.

What I've (through a lot of frustration) discovered is that there does not appear to be a way of breaking the connection between the name used for checkout and the name used for jarring. If there is, I would be thrilled to find it.

The solution may turn out to be as simple as letting this automated system build the way it wants to and then rename the files at the end. This would probably mean deleting the generated zip file and then rezipping with the renamed jar files.

So there are really two parts to my question. 1. Am I thinking of all this in the right way ... that is, am I trying to do something unconventional (I don't want to) and is there a more standard way of providing daily builds to users. And 2) if my goal is a conventional one, how do people interact with these automatically generated files to get the job done? 

-ted

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Jun  8 16:33:52 2007
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 08 Jun 2007 16:33:52 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Update: Accessing Motorola RAZR V3c cell phone
Message-ID: <4669E760.80400@comcast.net>

Some months ago I tried looking for a way to get pictures off my cell
phone without sending them using the cellular network. Moto4Lin didn't
work, nor was I able to find anything else. I got busy and let it drop
(the search, not the phone!).

Yesterday, I found myself wanting to be able to rely on my cell phone
camera as a backup camera, and still wanted the ability to download
images directly to my laptop (XP). I stopped in at one of the Verizon
stores and learned that the software and cable package they offer does
not grant access to the photos on my particular phone. That version of
the RAZR phone is locked down in such a way as to prevent anything
except being able to sync its address book with a copy kept by a
designed-for-the-purpose Windoze program.

Other versions of the RAZR line have other features. One has a memory
card slot that holds the pictures. I assume it can be read by any memory
card reader. (Since my phone doesn't have the slot, I didn't look into
it further.)

One day I'll upgrade to a TREO running Palm software (or something like
it -- maybe there'll be something better by the time I'm ready). When I
do, I'll try to remember not to purchase it tied to a phone company. I
suspect it might be more expensive than a locked phone, <rant>but I
really don't like the idea of some corporate philosophy deciding what I
can and can't do with hardware I purchase.</rant>

Regards,

Dick Steffens

I don't have a RAZR, but assuming it has Bluetooth, have you tried BitPim?
www.bitpim.org
Its a nice little program that works on Linux and Windoze.
I have used it with my Verizon locked LG Vx8100.  I got a cheapo USB
Bluetooth dongle (way cheaper than the proprietary connection cable and
proprietary Windoze only transfer program they have), and with BitPim and
this dongle I can transfer anything and everything I want (up to the mem
limit on the phone of course).  Address book, pics, and ringtones.
Not sure if it supports RAZR but it might be worth a shot.

On a side note, once you have more open and ready access to your phone, if
you are into this kinda thing, you can use an audio editor and trim your
favorite MP3s and use them as your own ringtones, instead of paying $1.99
per 3 or whatever they want to rip you off at.
I use Audacity, and have several favorites on my phone.  Available for
Linux/Mac/Windoze.
Usually just take out one of the stereo tracks, downsample the rate to
16khz, a few other tweaks, and it works great on the phone's tiny speakers.
audacity.sourceforge.net

From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Wed Jun  6 12:41:24 2007

Subject: [PLUG] How many ways to write a FQDN???
Message-ID: <1181158884.24935.0.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

How many ways can a fully qualified DNS domain name be written that
are resolveable?

I know of only this one:

some.name.com

Are there other possibilities that are common in SMTP email headers?

On the subject, what is the maximum nth level subdomain for DNS names?
I know it's possibly to get to 4 levels.  For example.

For some.name.com, it has a first level of the root domain, a second  
level of the .com sub domain, a third level of the .name subdomain,  
and fourth level
of the some. host name.

If I know the longest name that DNS can do, that makes it potentially
possible to avoid doing unnecessary DNS searches on bogus FQDN strings.

RFC 1035 suggest 255 octets which means, I think, that a fully qualified
domain name can have up to 255 characters in it.  Whether that includes
the
periods or not, I'm not sure.

      Michael C. Robinson

Subject: [PLUG] DNS replacement proposal...
Message-ID: <1181189767.24935.32.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

http://www.cfp2000.org/workshop/materials/projects-dns.html

One thing I have against this idea is that anonymity on the
Web, if it ever becomes possible, will help spammers a lot.

Actually, I question the ability of people to find out 
where a site is located just off the domain name.  With
whois databases being cleared of useful information, 
how else can you track a domain to it's source?

How many words are there in the English language that are
appropriate names for a web site?  Considering that names
don't mean the same thing in all countries, the list gets
shorter doesn't it?  How about other languages, can DNS
support different alphabets?

What percentage of the available names are used up?

If a registar delegates someone the foo.org domain,
but later wants to delegate both the abe.foo.org 
and ben.foo.org domains, what can it do?

If fully qualified DNS domain names can be up to 255
characters long including the periods, how many 
domains is that really?  How many unique domains are
there that actually exist on the Net now?

From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Wed Jun  6 23:14:40 2007
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Wed, 06 Jun 2007 23:14:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Programming postfix mail log...
Message-ID: <1181196880.24935.70.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

An excerpt from a Postfix-2.2 mail log:

May 27 04:29:50 web postfix/smtpd[29353]: NOQUEUE: reject: RCPT from
sophistasis.com[209.210.212.134]: 550 <plug_0 at robinson-west.com>:
Recipient address rejected: User unknown in relay recipient table;
from=<jodimsamericalaq at imsamerica.com> to=<plug_0 at robinson-west.com>
proto=SMTP helo=<sophistasis.com>

Did my smtp server or the remote one provide the following???
     RCPT from sophistasis.com[209.210.212.134]:

On the subject of keeping spammers from using my plug
email address, is there a simple way to get a list
of the subscribers to plug?

What will Postfix 2.2 log for a helo, mail from, and RCPT from?
My Perl script searches entries like the above excerpt on the 
assumption that: <,>, and = will never show up in a helo, 
RCPT from, or mail from.  Can I ever get something like:

helo=<<>> 

or

RCPT from=<=>

or

to=<<=>>?

The following Perl code is from my script.

...
$badstr=  ( *NOTE: I had to break the line here * )
": Recipient address rejected: User unknown in relay recipient table";
...
if ( $trouble_line=~/$badstr/ )
{
# ******************************************************************
# From a trouble line extract the client helo, client RCPT From,
# and client ip address.

# Extract the helo/ehlo address from the original $trouble_line.
$helofrom=$trouble_line;
$helofrom=~s/^.*;.*> proto=.* helo=<//;
$helofrom=~s/>.*//;
chomp ($helofrom);

# Extract client RCPT from...
$rcptfrom=$trouble_line;
$rcptfrom=~s/^.*RCPT from //;
$rcptfrom=~s/\]: 550 .*//;

# Before finishing $rcptfrom, extract the client ip address...
$rcptfip=$rcptfrom;
$rcptfip=~s/^.*\[//;

# Take ip address off of $rcptfrom...
$rcptfrom=~s/\[[0-9.]*//;
chomp ($rcptfrom);
chomp ($rcptfip);
# ******************************************************************


# Extract the mail from address from the original
# $trouble_line.

$msgfrom=$trouble_line;
$msgfrom=~s/^.*RCPT from .*\[[0-9.]*\]: 550 .*;//;
$msgfrom=~s/ proto=.* helo=<.*//;

# Extract the mail to address from the current $msgfrom.
$msgto=$msgfrom;
$msgto=~s/ from=<.*> to=<//;
$msgto=~s/>.*//;

# Finish extracting the mail from address.
$msgfrom=~s/ from=<//;
$msgfrom=~s/> to=<.*//;

chomp ($msgto);
chomp ($msgfrom);
...
}
...

My question is, will the above code always work?  Are
the regular expressions I'm using above ever going to
get tripped up?

Please limit answers to Perl.

NOTE: This code is in a loop reading a Postfix 2.2 
      mail log a line at a time into $trouble_line.
      The outer loop and code to read a file are 
      cut.

    -- Michael Robinson

HERE ?????.kornet.net/ at ./block_smtp.pl line 152, <INDAT> line 16249.

What follows is the comparison that crashed:
  $domtotry =~ /$helofrom/

It's the fist time I've seen a log of a spammers rejected email message
where the helo string has a series of question marks in it.  So how can
I deal with this?  Is there a way to change the comparison above so that
Perl knows that $helofrom is not to be interpreted as having any special
characters?

      Michael C. Robinson

From bobby_miesen at yahoo.com  Fri Jun  8 22:55:08 2007
From: bobby_miesen at yahoo.com (Robert Jeffrey Miesen)
Date: Fri, 08 Jun 2007 22:55:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] About to start developing a new Graphing (as in mathematical
 graphs) program
Message-ID: <466A40BC.7030907@yahoo.com>

Hi all.


    I'm currently in the planning phase of designing a new graphing 
application (as in the discrete math kind, not the calculus kind). My 
primary intent in writing this application is to create a GUI front-end 
for a graph library that extends the boost C++ library to suit more 
specific kinds of graphs (like, say UML or E-RD.) This isn't meant to be 
a clone of Visio, but is rather meant to focus on providing a front-end 
for users to do graphical analysis on their graphs (such as finding the 
number of cycles in their graphs, getting the number of outgoing and 
ingoing connections between nodes (where a node can be either just a 
plain old node or a specialized node like a UML class or a database 
table), auto-balancing graphs based on the number of ingoing and 
outgoing connections, finding the shortest paths and so on and so forth. 
As I envision it, these functions can have special meaning inside 
certain modes (such as Plain Old graph mode, UML mode, or ERD mode or 
whatever other modes there may be).


    Architecture wise, I currently plan to write this application in C++ 
using QT4 and possibly the KDE widget set (whatever it's called). I wish 
to use this architecture because C++ is a stable, proven language that 
is supported DFSG-wise (Debian Free Software Guidelines) across every 
major platform (including Windows---an important consideration in this 
case.) I wish to use QT4 and, if it's multi-platform compatible, the KDE 
libs and widgets (I honestly don't know, namely because I've never tried 
to compile KDE under Windows) because they are:


   1. Well designed: They implement the standard composite design
      pattern both correctly and in a manner that can be both easily and
      effectively exploited in the originally-supported language (for
      those who don't know, GUI widget sets tend to be designed using a
      Composite pattern, which enables widgets to be built in a
      hierarchy of widgets, which consist of Leafs and Components. A
      Leaf cannot have any Leafs or Components, but a Component (like a
      frame widget) can hold either more Components or Leafs. For a more
      detailed discussion of the Composite design pattern, look it up by
      name in "Design Patterns: Elements of Reusable Object-Oriented
      Software" or check it out at
      http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composite_pattern.)

   2. Operating system-independently free: As long as the program being
      written is licensed under the GPL licensing scheme (which it will
      be), your program can be distributed freely, under both Linux
      /and/ Windows (see
      http://trolltech.com/developer/downloads/qt/windows.)

   3. Stable and reliable: Both C++ and QT4 have been around for a long
      time and both appear to be quite stable and reliable: both from a
      design perspective and a QA perspective.


    I also plan to draw certain design ideas from a few existing 
graphing-like applications. Namely:

    * Kivio: The front-end they use for their graphing library. It is
      nicely done and a lot can (and should) be drawn from it.

    * Umbrello: It automates certain things about building UML diagrams.
      Namely, they automate the implementation of abstract methods and
      (should) auto-inherit public and protected methods. These would be
      useful generalize and allow the user to decide if and when they
      want these automated features active (like, for example, I
      shouldn't be forced to have aggregations going to diagram-defined
      classes when I've specified them as attributes of a class, even if
      it's "correct").

    * Visio: It implements custom nodes of some sort and should probably
      be looked at.

    * PIGALE: It seems to do a lot of the stuff I'm talking about, and a
      few concepts not really seen in most graphing programs, such as
      multi-dimensional graphs. This should be looked into by all means.


    I'm posting a notification of this here because this is my first 
major project---open source or otherwise---and I could use some advice 
from a veteran open source software developer on the logistical and 
pragmatic ends of open source development. I'm also looking for anyone 
interested in joining me in developing this graphing program. I've 
already submitted this project for consideration for hosting at 
sourceforge.net and, if all goes well, it will be approved soon. I'd 
also like feedback in general on my ideas in general. Is what I'm 
planning something others would be interested in or am I just 
reinventing the wheel?

    Thanks in advance for all your support!


From bobby_miesen at yahoo.com  Fri Jun  8 23:10:03 2007
From: bobby_miesen at yahoo.com (Robert Jeffrey Miesen)
Date: Fri, 08 Jun 2007 23:10:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Perl regular expression crashing...
In-Reply-To: <20070608133043.7nq7cpwj6s0gg04g@web.robinson-west.com>
References: <20070608133043.7nq7cpwj6s0gg04g@web.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <466A443B.3060102@yahoo.com>

Yes, there's a way to turn off all meta-characters for a given segment 
of a regular expression. Here's some code w/ comments



********************START CODE SEGMENT********************

#!/usr/bin/perl -w


  # setup a scenario similar to what made your code go BOOM
$strWithQuestionMarks = '?????';
$anotherStr = "Another stirng";

  # now stop PERL from going BOOM with the \Q and \E meta-characters!
$anotherStr =~ /\Q$strWithQuestionMarks\E/;

  ##  RJM:  \QTurns off the interpretation of ALL meta-characters until 
either
  ##             \E is reached or the end of the given regex is reached.

print "HA HA! I worked! :p\n\n\n";

********************END CODE SEGMENT********************

    If you need any more help, just say so. :)

From linux at frankhunt.com  Sat Jun  9 03:42:02 2007
From: linux at frankhunt.com (Frank Hunt)
Date: Sat, 09 Jun 2007 06:42:02 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] How many ways to write a FQDN???
In-Reply-To: <1181158884.24935.0.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1181158884.24935.0.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <466A83FA.5010101@frankhunt.com>

How about co.washington.or.us. ?

You are correct in that the maximum permitted length of an FQDN is 255 
bytes, with an additional restriction to 63 bytes for each label within 
the domain name.  So, yes - it does include the periods (4 * 63 +3 = 255)

someone wrote:
> How many ways can a fully qualified DNS domain name be written that
> are resolveable?
> 
> I know of only this one:
> 
> some.name.com
> 
> Are there other possibilities that are common in SMTP email headers?
> 
> On the subject, what is the maximum nth level subdomain for DNS names?
> I know it's possibly to get to 4 levels.  For example.
> 
> For some.name.com, it has a first level of the root domain, a second  
> level of the .com sub domain, a third level of the .name subdomain,  
> and fourth level
> of the some. host name.
> 
> If I know the longest name that DNS can do, that makes it potentially
> possible to avoid doing unnecessary DNS searches on bogus FQDN strings.
> 
> RFC 1035 suggest 255 octets which means, I think, that a fully qualified
> domain name can have up to 255 characters in it.  Whether that includes
> the
> periods or not, I'm not sure.
> 
>       Michael C. Robinson

Does anyone know of an application, either Linux or Windows, that can
view and print a scanned, multi-page document saved as a .tif file? I'm
used to such things being saved as .pdf. The sender says that he's
sending me a MS Word document. It includes signatures, so it is an
image, as opposed to just text, so I'm not completely surprised at the
.tif format.

I've tried opening the file with the GIMP (both the Windows version and
the Linux version). I watch the progress bar zip by enough times to
account for the 12 pages that are supposed to be in the document, but I
only see what I assume to be the last page. Page Up and Page Down change
the page number shown at the bottom of the image display. I note that
Page Up and Page Down are supposed to switch between layers, but the
image stays the same.

I've tried opening the document with OO Writer, using File-Open. A
single, blank slide opens in OpenOffice.org Draw.

Hopefully the fellow who sent me the file can simply fax it, and I'll
get what I need. But, it would be nice to know what this is all about. I
stopped buying MS Office at Office 97. Did MS decide to support .tif's
as an alternative to .pdf for document images?

In-Reply-To: <4669ED30.3090104@comcast.net>
